::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[8/8/75](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle227){#e9781466802179_c12.html_title227}
:::

::: p
Art Deco the last "international"---total---style. (From fine arts to furniture, everyday objects, clothes, etc.) All styles in the last 50 years have been comments on Art Deco. E.g. Art Deco straightened out, made rectilinear the vertiginous curves of Art Nouveau, the next to last international style; Bauhaus (Mies \[van der Rohe\], \[Philip\] Johnson, etc.) banned all ornament; but the structure remains the same.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Fascist architecture: parody + Art Deco (\[Albert\] Speer, "Mussolini")
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Why has there been no new international style in 50 years? Because the new ideas, the new needs are not yet clear. (Hence, we content ourselves with variations + refinements on Art Deco and, for refreshment + fusions, parodistic---"pop"---revivals of older styles.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A new style will emerge in the last decade of this century, with the ascendancy of the ecological crisis---and possibility of eco-fascism
:::

\

:::: blocktext
::: p-br-blocktext
Low buildings\
Caves\
No windows\
Stone
:::

<?dp n="412" folio="396" ?>
::::

\

::: p
The skyscraper will seem like hubris, + it will be impractical
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Most influential "painter" of our century: Duchamp. Dissolves the idea of art
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Most influential poet: Mallarmé. Advances the idea of the difficult writer. There have always been difficult writers (e.g. ancient distinction between esoteric and exoteric texts) but no one before had ever advanced difficulty---i.e. purity---i.e. elimination of content---as the criterion of value. Mallarmé invented the idea (not the practice) which has been influential in a way that no practice ever could be.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
1910s---[art]{.i} inherited political rhetoric (that of Anarchism) cf. Marinetti
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
1960s---[feminism]{.i} inherited political rhetoric (that of gauchisme) against hierarchy, intellect (as bourgeois, phallo-centric, repressive), the theoretical
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
rigged hopes, rigged despair
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The "thou" which the self needs for its own fulfillment
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Power of art = power to negate
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

fiction: schemes of enlightenment and redemption

::: p-blanc

:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[9/4/75 NC](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle228){#e9781466802179_c12.html_title228}

:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
PLEASURE---I have forgotten the rights of pleasure. Sexual pleasure. Getting pleasure out of my writing, and using pleasure as one criterion for what I choose to write.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I am an adversary writer, a polemical writer. I write to support what is attacked, to attack what is acclaimed. But thereby I put myself in an emotionally uncomfortable position. I don't, secretly, hope to convince, and can't help being dismayed when my minority taste (ideas) becomes majority taste (ideas): then I want to attack again. I can't help but be in an adversary relation to my own work.
:::

<?dp n="414" folio="398" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The interesting writer is where there is an adversary, a problem. Why Stein is not, finally, a good or helpful writer. There is no problem. It's all affirmation. A rose is a rose is a rose.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Since Biblical times, to be connected with people sexually is a way of knowing them. In our century---for the first time---it is valued primarily as a way of knowing oneself. That's too much of a burden for the sexual act to carry.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...

... Fits of lucidity
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Grief can drive one mad
:::

::: p-blanc

::: p
Foucault has wanted to do an essay on cemeteries---as utopias
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
... Every situation is defined by the amount of energy one puts into it---I put so much energy into my love, my hope---I am moved to put an equal amount into my grief, my sense of loss.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I must think about David---Yuyi \[[an Argentine friend in Paris in the period]{.i}\] said (rightly) that I don't describe him, I describe my relationship with him (us)---when she asked me to describe him, I felt blocked---embarrassed---as she were inviting me to describe the best part of myself. That's the key to the problem: I identify myself too much with him, him too much with myself. What a burden for him---all that admira-ton, that confidence that I feel for (in) him.
:::

<?dp n="417" folio="401" ?>

::: p
I am convalescent---je \[me\] traîne---I'm looking for new sources of energy.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The German-American literary critic]{.i}\] Erich Kahler wrote of \[Thomas\] Mann ten years before his death: "He is someone who feels a personal responsibility for the human condition."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
... Yes, I am a Puritan. Twice over---American and Jew
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
It's not "natural" to speak well, eloquently, in an interesting articulate way. People living in groups, families, communes say little---have few verbal means. Eloquence---thinking in words---is a byproduct of solitude, deracination, a heightened painful individuality. In groups, it's more natural to sing, to dance, to pray: given, rather than invented (individual) speech.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::: p-blanc
 \

[6/19/76 New York](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle234){#e9781466802179_c13.html_title234}

::: p
I returned Sunday night. Have been meditating helplessly, suffering compulsively. I squirm like a pinned insect. There is no help for it. I am afraid, paralyzed. I need:
:::

\

:::: blocktext
::: p-br-blocktext
Energy\
Humility\
Obstinacy\
Discipline
:::
::::

\

::: p
All these together = courage.
:::

::: p-indent
Note that obstinacy + discipline are not the same. I have often been obstinate but I have no discipline at all.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Not only must I summon the courage to be a bad writer---I must dare to be truly unhappy. Desperate. And not save myself, short-circuit the despair.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
By refusing to be as unhappy as I truly am, I deprive myself of subjects. I've nothing to write about. Every topic burns.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

... Changes in the body, changes in language, changes in the sense of time. What does it mean for time to go faster, for it to seem to pass more slowly?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Jasper's observation that the reason time seems to go faster + faster as we get older is that we think in larger units. At forty, it's as easy to say "in five years" or "five years ago," as it was to say "in five months" or "five months ago" when one was fourteen.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Brodsky said there were two subjects: time and language.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[9/3/76 Paris](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle237){#e9781466802179_c13.html_title237}
:::

::: p
Resemblance between J.-K. Huysmans' [Là-Bas]{.i} \[[Down There]{.i}\], published in 1891, and \[J. G.\] Ballard's [Crash]{.i}, published in 1973
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Both are about Satanism; both describe and celebrate a Black Mass; both describe search for a metallic, transhuman sexuality;---but for Huysmans the tradition was already there, indeed it dated back to the Middle Ages, while for Ballard it is a "new" post-modern or futuristic sexuality or diabolism.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Both reject the modern.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Both acclaim the violation (self-violation) of the body.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Common sense (le bon sens) is always wrong. It is the demagoguery of the bourgeois ideal. The function of common sense is to simplify, to reassure, to hide unpleasant truths and mysteries. I don't just mean that this is what common sense does, or ends up doing; I mean this is what it is designed to do. Of course, in order to be effective common sense must contain some part of the truth. But its main content is negative: To say (implicitly) that, this being so, that is not so.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Similarly, all polls of opinion must be superficial. They reveal the top of what people think, organized into common sense. What people really think is always partly hidden.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Only way to get at it is through a study of their language---a study in depth: its metaphors, structures, tone. And of their gestures, way of moving in space.
:::

<?dp n="423" folio="407" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
All orthodoxy, whether religious or political, is an enemy of language; all orthodoxy postulates "the usual expression."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Novalis' definition of Romanticism: to make the familiar appear strange, the marvelous appear commonplace
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Beckett found a new subject for the drama:---what am I going to do in the next second? Weep, take out my comb, sigh, sit, be silent, tell a joke, die ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

Nonsense! Modernist-nihilist-wise-guy bullshit.

Writing means converting one's liabilities (limitations) into advantages. For example, I don't love what I'm writing. Okay, then---that's also a way to write, a way that can produce interesting results.

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[11/12/76](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle239){#e9781466802179_c13.html_title239}
:::

::: p
Technological reproduction not simply an "era," as Benjamin says. That's misleading. It has its history---rather, \[it\] is inserted into history. Its artifacts become "historical," not merely contemporary. Old litho\[graphs\], photos, comic books, movies, etc. are redolent of the past, not the present. B\[enjamin\] thought tech. rep\[roduction\] made everything into an eternal present---a Hegelian end-of-history (and abolition of history). Another four decades of living in this "era" has disproved this.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The range of recollection of a writer's work.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Poetry is the enunciation of universality---some poet said
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
[Taste]{.i} is contrapuntal, reactive (definition of taste)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Style comes into existence, only as it discovers a subject. True?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The Austrian art historian]{.i}\] Alois Riegl (on form + design in industrial arts)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"This is [not]{.i} a subject: one delicate sensibility confronting the slimy, heartless, disappointing world. Go get yourself an agon." (me to Sigrid \[[the American writer Sigrid Nunez]{.i}\].)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

:::

::: p-blanc

:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

[]{#e9781466802179_c14.html}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: chapter
::: title-chapter
[[1977]{.b}](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle242){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title242}
:::

::: p
"If you want to be quoted, don't quote." (JB \[Joseph Brodsky\])
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"All art aspires to the condition of music"---this utterly nihilistic statement rests at the foundation of every moving camera style in the history of the medium. But it is a cliché, a 19th c\[entury\] cliché, less an aesthetic than a projection of an exhausted state of mind, less a world view than a world weariness, less a statement of vital forms than an expression of sterile decadence. There is quite another pov \[point of view\] about what "all art aspires to"---that was Goethe's, who put the primary art, the most aristocratic one, + the one art that cannot be made by the plebes but only gaped at w\[ith\] awe, + that art is architecture. Really great directors have this sense of architecture in their work---always expressive of immense line of energy, unstable + vital conduits of force.
:::

<?dp n="431" folio="415" ?>

::: p
Title for cancer / TB essay:
:::

::: p-br
"The Discourse of Illness"\
or\
"Illness as Metaphor"
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A good poem will have romantic form + modern content. (Brodsky)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
To think only about oneself is to think of death.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The egoism of modernism
:::

\

:::: blocktext
::: p-br-blocktext
fantasies\
solipsism
:::
::::

\

::: p
The novel (19th century \>) implies
:::

\

:::::: blocktext
::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
interest in the world (not solipsistic)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
ability to pass judgments on human behavior (moralistic)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
patience
:::
::::::

\

::: p
Proust (the biggest, greatest work of prose fiction straddles both worlds---is about the world and is about solipsism)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Novelist as moralist: Austen, \[George\] Eliot, Stendhal, Tolstoy, Dostoyevsky, Proust, DH L\[awrence\]
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Modernist novel comes into being when no judgment seems tenable (e.g. [Anna Karenina]{.i}: marriage is good, passion destroys). We always think of counter-examples.
:::

<?dp n="432" folio="416" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Tolstoyan conception of the novel has been abandoned to the dummies (James Michener, etc.) at the apex of which is Gore Vidal. The track record of modernism---the "art novel"---is infinitely better. But it's at a dead end. What we have now is a codified orthodoxy of modernism (John Barth, [Lost in the Funhouse]{.i}; Sarraute; Coover, [Pricksongs & Descants]{.i}---they're not writing about anything.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
Problem of writing a novel now: No story seems that important to tell.
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
Why?
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
Because we are unable to draw any moral (meaning: judgment) from it.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Tolstoy has subjects: the nature of marriage ([Anna Karenina]{.i}); of history, etc. ([War + Peace]{.i})
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
If no story\[, no\] narrative seems that important or necessary. The only material that seems to have any character of inevitability is the writer's own consciousness.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
18th century:
:::

\

:::::: blocktext
::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
"reason" not motivational
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
distinction between a sentiment and passion/ emotion; sentiments are calm passions (e.g. benevolence, self-interest, sympathy)---see \[the Earl of\] Shaftesbury, \[David\] Hume, and Rousseau
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
discovery of the plasticity of the emotions
:::
::::::

\

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] imagination as a moral faculty
:::

<?dp n="433" folio="417" ?>

::: p
Compare the Greeks:
:::

\

:::::: blocktext
::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
reason is motivational
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
emotions are of two types---those expressing the person + those understood as invasive, alien (we don't make this distinction---everything is "inner")
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
little emphasis on the plasticity of the emotions.
:::
::::::

\

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] Cf. \[Aristotle's\] [Nic]{.i}\[[omachean]{.i}\] [Ethics]{.i}
:::

::: p-blanc

::: p
Two experiences yesterday---lunch with \[[the English--West Indian writer V. S]{.i}.\] Naipaul and reading \[[the Russian formalist Boris]{.i}\] Eikhenbaum's [The Young Tolstoy]{.i}---remind me of how undisciplined and demoralized I am.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Starting tomorrow---if not today:
:::

\

::::::::: blocktext
::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will get up every morning no later than eight. (Can break this rule [once]{.i} a week.)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will have lunch only with Roger \[Straus\]. ("No, I don't go out for lunch." Can break this rule once every two weeks.)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will write in the Notebook every day. (Model: Lichtenberg's [Waste Books]{.i}.)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will tell people not to call in the morning, or not answer the phone.
:::

<?dp n="434" folio="418" ?>

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will try to confine my reading to the evening. (I read too much---as an escape from writing.)
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
I will answer letters once a week. (Friday?---I have to go to the hospital anyway.)
:::
:::::::::

::: p
Things I like: fires, Venice, tequila, sunsets, babies, silent films, heights, coarse salt, top hats, large long-haired dogs, ship models, cinnamon, goose down quilts, pocket watches, the smell of newly mown grass, linen, Bach, Louis XIII furniture, sushi, microscopes, large rooms, ups, boots, drinking water, maple sugar candy.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Things I dislike: sleeping in an apartment alone, cold weather, couples, football games, swimming, anchovies, mustaches, cats, umbrellas, being photographed, the taste of licorice, washing my hair (or having it washed), wearing a wristwatch, giving a lecture, cigars, writing letters, taking showers, Robert Frost, German food.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Things I like: ivory, sweaters, architectural drawings, urinating, pizza (the Roman bread), staying in hotels, paper clips, the color blue, leather belts, making lists, Wagon-Lits, paying bills, caves, watching ice-skating, asking questions, taking taxis, Benin art, green apples, office furniture, Jews, eucalyptus trees, pen knives, aphorisms, hands.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
Things I dislike: Television, baked beans, hirsute men, paperback books, standing, card games, dirty or disorderly apartments,
<?dp n="435" folio="419" ?>
flat pillows, being in the sun, Ezra Pound, freckles, violence in movies, having drops put in my eyes, meatloaf, painted nails, suicide, licking envelopes, ketchup, traversins \[["bolsters"]{.i}\], nose drops, Coca-Cola, alcoholics, taking photographs.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Things I like: drums, carnations, socks, raw peas, chewing on sugar cane, bridges, Dürer, escalators, hot weather, sturgeon, tall people, deserts, white walls, horses, electric typewriters, cherries, wicker / rattan furniture, sitting cross-legged, stripes, large windows, fresh dill, reading aloud, going to bookstores, under-furnished rooms, dancing, [Ariadne auf Naxos]{.i}.

::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[2/22/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle246){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title246}
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I'm polite to too many people because I'm not angry enough. I'm not angry enough because I don't push my ideas far enough. The comfortable refuge of "pluralism," "dialogue," etc.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
My refusal of intransigence. I lose energy thereby---every day.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The great intransigent arguments---SW \[Simone Weil\], Artaud, Adorno (in [The Philosophy of Modern Music]{.i}). I don't think I'm obliged to agree or disagree. They're my amphetamine, my points "de rigueur." I work in relation to those extremes, but, by self-definition---my own views are not extreme.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Too easy a way out? I'm not exerting myself.
:::

<?dp n="436" folio="420" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The great question of pleasure. How "serious" a view is one to have of it? To what extent do moral criteria apply? Nobody wants to be known as a puritan, and yet ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Cf. Adorno's denunciation of pleasure in music as morally corrupt, historically reactionary
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Didn't I feel this about \[[the American theater director Robert Wilson's opera]{.i}\] [Einstein on the Beach]{.i}---And yet I was pleased (glad) to be able to enjoy it.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Remember that Adorno is writing in 1940--41 (the awareness of Nazi horrors---and those unresolved; he is a refugee). The author of [The Philosophy of Modern Music]{.i} is the same person who wrote (in 1947) that there could be no poetry after Auschwitz. He would have said that in consumer society of Europe of the 60s.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
For "aesthetic way of looking at the world"---see Hugo Ball's [Flight Out of Time: A Dada Diary]{.i} ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Story Irene told me about being robbed + raped four years ago. In her building: as she was returning home about 1 a.m. getting into the elevator, a black man forced it open. She screamed. "If you scream again, I'll kill you." Took her to the eighth (top) floor, then halfway up stairs leading to the roof. Then blindfolded her.
:::

<?dp n="437" folio="421" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I asked, "Did you get excited sexually?" She said yes---then said I was the first person she'd ever told that story to who'd asked her that. "But it's such an obvious question," I said.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The next day (today) I called her. "I was saying how stupid your friends are," I said, "but I was thinking now that it was because you told me it happened four years ago---+ you obviously were OK, not traumatized, talked about it so coolly---that it was easy to ask that."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[2/25/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle248){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title248}
:::

::: p
University of Chicago education: no idea of "the modern." Texts, ideas, arguments---exist in a timeless dialogue. The basic themes or questions are those stated by Plato and Aristotle (relation of theory and practice; one or many sciences; relation of virtue and knowledge, etc. etc.) and the moderns are interesting, valuable so far as they too discuss those themes. (We read Bentham, Mill, Dewey, \[Rudolf\] Carnap.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The most radical opposite to the timeless, which starts with the category of "the modern." The basic themes or questions are those stated at the beginning of the modern era (by Rousseau; Hegel) and previous thinkers are interesting, valuable so far as they contrast with the moderns.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
With historicist approach, you ask different questions. (Historicism [changes]{.i} the questions---and destroys the themes.) As N\[ietzsche\] saw, historicism is a fundamentally destructive
<?dp n="438" folio="422" ?>
pov \[point of view\]. For example, Foucault: the very subject of the human sciences ("man") is destroyed.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[4/19/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle250){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title250}
:::

::: p
Clear = what one already knows
:::

::: p
Obscure = a meaning one doesn't want to attend to
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Copying out ten pages from \[Proust's\] [Le Temps Retrouvé]{.i} (to imprint them---like books one reads before the age of fifteen):
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Proust didn't know he was writing the greatest novel ever written. (Neither did his contemporaries, even the most admiring, like Rivière.) And it wouldn't have done him any good if he had. But he did want to write something great.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I want to write something great.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I'm not ambitious enough. (It's not just a question of becoming truly intransigent.) I want to be good, liked, etc. I'm afraid of allowing real feeling, real arrogance, selfishness.
:::

<?dp n="441" folio="425" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I want to sing.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I said it already, in the first thing I wrote for [PR]{.i} \[[Partisan Review]{.i}\] on \[[the Yiddish- language writer Isaac Bashevis]{.i}\] Singer. To hell with modern catatonia.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I have more than enough intelligence, learning, vision. The obstacle is character: boldness.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Ruthlessness
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Duchamp: too smart to be a painter, like Leonardo; but destroys, parodies---instead of constructs. Leonardo, the great constructor; Duchamp, the great de-constructor. Same fascination with machines, but Duchamp's is entirely playful, nihilistic ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::::: section1
::: title-section1
[7/12/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle251){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title251}

::: p
Project: convert my photographer's eye (mute) into a poet's eye, which hears---words. I see concretely; I write abstractly. The project: to have access, as a writer, to that concreteness. The clot of light on Bob S.'s \[Robert Silvers\] nose at dinner tonight in the Indian restaurant.
:::
:::::

:::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[7/19/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle252){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title252}
:::

::: p
Story about a sorcerer (female)
:::

<?dp n="442" folio="426" ?>

::: p
What is most American about me (Emerson, etc.) is my faith in the possibility of radical change.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joseph \[Brodsky\] said that when he began writing he consciously competed with other poets. Now I'll write a poem that will be better (more profound) than \[Boris\] Pasternak (or \[Anna\] Akhmatova---or Frost---or Yeats---or Lowell, etc.) And now? I asked. "Now I'm arguing with angels."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The importance of being envious, competitive. I don't try hard enough.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
After THE LAST PHONE CALL FROM NICOLE, tonight
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Let it hurt, let it hurt.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
So this isn't my front door any longer. Then walk away.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Remember: this could be my one chance, and the last, to be a first-rate writer.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
One can never be alone enough to write. To see better.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In a sense---in one sense---I was wasting my time the last three years w\[ith\] Nicole. I knew that---still wanted to do it. Now that that possibility is no longer available to me, though ...
:::

::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[9/8/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle257){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title257}

::: p

:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
4-page weekly newspaper I wrote and published (Hectograph) and sold for 5¢ a copy when I was 9, 10, 11 years old.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
Fear of---irritation with---images in conversation. I'm already thinking, visualizing one thing; abruptly, I'm made to see something else. \[[The American writer]{.i}\] Walker Percy telling me how to get to his house from New Orleans. "Take the Pontchartrain Bridge---26 miles---straight as a string." I'm visualizing the bridge, the plantation house, the bayou, the moss-covered trees. Suddenly there's this damned string ... Paul \[Thek\] today, talking about sexual attitudes. "And that's
<?dp n="445" folio="429" ?>
the bottom card." Later, slumping down, his arms dangling. "They cut my strings." (More strings!)`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: verse

::: source
---Sontag
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Bresson, in [Notes on Cinematography]{.i}, quotes Leonardo as saying: in an artistic context all that matters is the end.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Athletes, dancers---having a romance with their bodies
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Apollinaire compares Eiffel Tower + Paris roofs to a shepherd + sheep. Image that reduces things to their geography.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The cave of the self.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Emily Dickinson said that "Art is a house that tries to be haunted."
:::

::: p
Now it doesn't have to try.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
So it's a question of time---when the image comes. It should precede or be simultaneous w\[ith\] the picture. Otherwise, it's distracting.

::::::::: section1

[9/26/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle260){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title260}
:::

::: p
<span>
Conversation with \[[the American painter R. B]{.i}.\] Kitaj at Bob's \[Robert Silvers\]. Spoke of "transcendent" art. Not possible if one doesn't know how to draw (and that's no longer taught in art schools). Last great painters were Picasso + Matisse. Best living painter(s): Bacon (+ Balthus). Only interested in depictive painting, figure painting. Whole 19th century French tradition referred itself to Ingres---Impressionism not possible without him. And who can draw like that now? Among contemporaries like Lucien Freud, Frank Auerbach, David Hockney---in England; de Kooning only American painter he
<?dp n="448" folio="432" ?>
mentioned. "But what are we talking about if we think of Rembrandt? And that's the standard against which we should be measuring contemporary painters." Also: many painters did their best work when they were old---Michelangelo, Titian, Goya, Tintoretto, Rembrandt, Turner, Monet, Matisse, maybe (despite current view) Picasso.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Painting as craft.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Don Barthelme: "I don't need a rule to tell me that I musn't strangle swans ..." After watching the thief from the Jeans Store being caught---"I hope they're not going to lynch him with tied-together jeans."
:::

[10/11/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle261){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title261}

::: p
Story Sonia Orwell told me today about the daughter of a very minor Politburo member---about to be married---"I don't want another boring Moscow wedding!"---500 guests flown in gov\[ernmen\]t planes to a Caspian Sea villa where wedding was held---footmen in livery (breeches, stockings, etc.), a servant behind every guest's chair, maids in white caps---the regalia of the ancien régime in 1977. Were the servants cynical, or were they enjoying themselves.
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/4/77 Venice \[[SS had come to attend the Venice Biennale]{.i}\]](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle263){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title263}
:::

::: p
Clear day, cleansing cold---the night comes early---I've never seen Venice more beautiful.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The Italian writer Alberto]{.i}\] Moravia met me at the airport; \[[the British poet and SS's friend]{.i}\] Stephen Spender was just leaving. First dinner with \[[the French poet and essayist]{.i}\] Claude Roy + \[[the French actress and playwright]{.i}\] Loleh Bellon + Geörgy Konrád (Hungarian writer) in Do Pozzi Hotel, after an hour at Florian's \[café\]. Joseph's reading at the Teatro Ateneo from 9--11 p.m. I had shivers when he stood up and declaimed his poems. He chanted, he sobbed; he looked magnificent. Boris Godunov; Gregorian chant; Hebrew moan. After, 2nd dinner with Joseph and walk. Then to Hotel Europa for the first time, at 2 p.m. N\[icole\]'s call!
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Nietzsche's main subject (?) was genius. He knew what genius was; he understood its pride, its euphoric states, its megalomania, its purity, its ruthlessness. He made it into a theory of history. (Subsequently the Germans made it into a politics.) He thought he was a genius, but, unlike Shakespeare and Michelangelo, he never wrote the Great Work. [Zarathustra]{.i} is his worst book; it's kitsch. The great N\[ietzsche\] is in the essays---mostly fragments.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
Two kinds of writers. Those who think this life is all there is, and want to describe everything: the fall, the battle, the accouchement, the horse-race. That is, Tolstoy. And those who think this life is a kind of testing-ground (for what we don't
<?dp n="451" folio="435" ?>
know---to see how much pleasure + pain we can bear or what pleasure + pain are?) and want to describe only the essentials. That is, Dostoyevsky. The two alternatives. How can one write like T. after D.? The task is to be as good as D.---as serious spiritually, + then go on from there.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
But, to give credit to Tolstoy, he knew something was wrong. Hence, he ended by repudiating his great novels. Couldn't meet his own spiritual demands as an [artist]{.i}. So he renounced art for action (a spiritual life). D. never could have repudiated his art, for moral reasons, because he knew how to reach a higher spiritual level with his art.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Only thing that counts are ideas. Behind ideas are \[moral\] principles. Either one is serious or one is not. Must be prepared to make sacrifices. I'm not a liberal.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Something wrong with the idea of "dissident" art. It's defined by the authorities. Abstract painting is dissident in the USSR, the art of large corporations in the US; in Poland it's been tolerated, even fashionable for twenty years. Nothing contestataire in itself about any content (?) or any style, e.g. abstract [or]{.i} figurative.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
No panel discussions or debates here at the Biennale. Just papers---not coordinated with each other, mimeographed copies of which are distributed the minute after the speaker stops.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Solzhenitsyn is a genuinely epic writer; also completely eclectic in style (uses 19th c. language, Party language, etc.). Mixes genres: soc\[ialist\] realist novel, essay, satire, tirade, Dostoyevskian philosophical novel. His greatness depends on that scope.
:::

<?dp n="452" folio="436" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joke about Fidel \[Castro\] addressing rally right after Revolution, urging everyone to get to work + build socialism. "Trabajo si, rumba no." \[["Work yes, rumba no."]{.i}\] And the crowd roars back: "Trabajo si, rumba no. Tra-ba-jo si, rum-ba no. Tra-ba-jo-si, rum-ba-no."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joseph: "Censorship is good for writers. For three reasons. One, it unites the whole nation as (or into) readers. Two, it gives the writer limits, something to push against. Three, it increases metaphoric powers of the language (the greater the censorship, the more Aesopian the writing must become)."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The status of the Jews in one brief joke. A. They're given an order to kill all the Jews + all the barbers. B. Why the barbers?
:::

György Konrád looks so much like Jacob \[Taubes\]---as soon as I saw him yesterday afternoon, I was attracted + repelled; and this morning, late breakfast à deux at Florian's later joined by Joseph---I discover that, of course, he was the man with whom Susan \[Taubes\] had an affair when she was in Budapest in August 1969.

::: p
Influence of \[[the Russian-born French writer]{.i}\] Boris Souvarine on Simone Weil. Souvarine wrote a book denouncing Stalin in 1934---was rejected by Malraux, the reader at Gallimard, with these words: "Vous et vos amis avez raison, Souvarine, et je serai de vos côtes quand vous êtes les plus forts." \[["You and your friends are right, Souvarine, and I will be on your side when you're the strongest."]{.i}\] (Book not published in France until 1938.)
:::

 \

\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] [The three evils---misogyny (sexism), anti-Semitism, and anti-intellectualism---against which I struggle]{.i}.

::: p
Prisoners in the camps \[[the Soviet Gulag]{.i}\] speak a lot about absolutes---futile absolutes. The greatest fuck in the world. The metal that can cut through any prison bars (strip of metal in the soles of shoes issued in the 1950s). The other side of powerlessness.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

An artist should be professional enough to be able to do anything well.

<?dp n="454" folio="438" ?>

::: p-blanc

::: p
The exiled writer from Eastern Europe. Here, in the West, nothing menaces but everything is hostile.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joseph: "Then I realized what I am. I am somebody who took the idea of individuality literally." His Eugene Onegin side again.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"Courage" is a word one can only use in the third person. Can't say "I am brave / courageous." Can say she or he is. It's a word about actions, a way of interpreting behavior. It does not describe any subjective state. "Fear," par contre, is a first person adjective. Can say / feel "I am afraid."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Many Russians get out now by marrying Jews. In the Soviet Union Jews are a means of transport.
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/6/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle265){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title265}
:::

::: p
The smell of wet stones. The rain. The lapping of the water against the "fondamenta" \[["a street parallel to a canal"]{.i}\]. The peaceful groan of the vaporetto as it starts up. The fog. The sound of footsteps. Seven gondolas like black crows; parked in the narrow canal, waddling, lolling.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Only negative ideas are useful. "Ideas are a means of transport. Only ideas which are a means of transport are my concern."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
One feels "I wrote a bad story" but not "I wrote a good story." The latter is for the others. At most one feels, I didn't write a bad book ... The same for courage. One doesn't feel "I was brave." One feels "I wasn't afraid." Or, at least it didn't show. I didn't act on my fear.
:::

<?dp n="455" folio="439" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
He \[Claude Roy\] is tired. He's known everybody.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The poet-in-exile \[Brodsky\], born in Leningrad, walking alone on the wet empty streets at two in the morning. It reminds him, "a little bit," of Leningrad.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Even though I feel like one, I'm not an only child. So my mother's narcissism, absences, inability to nurture was less damaging than it might have been. I saw she did it even more to my sister. I didn't take it "personally." I could say: I have [this]{.i} kind of mother. Not: she treats me badly, she doesn't love me because I'm not / don't have (these qualities). From an early age I learned to be "objective."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
When I understand something completely, it goes dead. Hence, I am drawn to "exile." Being at home means knowing at each step what is possible. Events have an underpinning, a cushion of the possible. You turn the corner and you are not surprised.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Instead of "dissident" art, "non-authorized" or "unauthorized" art?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
All political language is alienated. Political language as such is the enemy. (Joseph's position)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A world in which there are dissidents everywhere + they are free. Or a world in which dissidence is no longer necessary (i.e. a good society). These are the two ideals presumed here---entirely opposite.
:::

::: p-blanc

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1

[12/7/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle266){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title266}
:::

::: p
What we call nihilism (now) I simply thought. What thinking doesn't lead to nihilism?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Everyone talks of rights (human rights, etc) ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
... There is only social thinking (accepting "society") or individualism---a profoundly asocial view of the world.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The lonely figures everywhere---many of whom wouldn't have liked each other---who uphold the asocial position. Oscar Wilde. Benjamin. Adorno. Cioran.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
True that Benjamin used a communist language in the last years of his life, so he looks different to us now. But that's because he died in 1940. Those last years were the ones in which communist language regained authority---seen as necessary to fight fascism (identified as The Enemy). Had Benjamin lived as long as Adorno \[he\] w\[oul\]d have become as a-social, as disillusioned with left as Adorno did.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
(Dinner with Joseph + Roberto Calasso, head of Adelphi Publishers in Milan. Lunch was with \[[the Polish theater critic]{.i}\] Jan Kott + \[[the scholar of Russian Literature]{.i}\] Victor Erlich. Breakfast was with \[[the Swiss journalist]{.i}\] François + \[his wife\] Lillian Bondy.)
:::

::: p
<span>
Roberto Calasso's story about John Cage's recent performance in Milan---2[1/2]{.fraction} hours of nonsense syllables drawn from a Thoreau text---before an audience of 2000 people in the Lirico, biggest theater in Milan. Almost a lynching. It started after 20 minutes. At one point there were a hundred people on the stage---someone put a blindfold on Cage then took it off.
<?dp n="457" folio="441" ?>
No one left. And throughout Cage never moved, went on reading at the table on the stage. Everyone cheered. It was a triumph.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Cage wants to put some emptiness in the middle of all the sense. \[[In the margin, SS repeats "the sense" and adds an exclamation point.]{.i}\] He's not a musician but a genial destroyer. The empty cage.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
When there is no censorship the writer has no importance.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
So it's not so simple to be against censorship.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A lateral idea
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The rhetorics of communism + nihilism. People who want to be good + \[people\] who want to be bad are both going in the same direction.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Both Marx + Freud were wrong. The one who was right is Malthus. Whatever happens, what's in front is a more repressive society ... The 19th century wouldn't recognize the society we live in.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

...

<?dp n="458" folio="442" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The fog. Standing in front of Museo Correr, looking across the Piazza San Marco and not seeing the Basilica. A new, surreal Venice in the fog: cut in segments, and then reassembled with the "far" parts missing (some of the parts missing).
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/8/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle267){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title267}
:::

::: p
Spiritual exercises: lowering the ideas into the body. Making it part of one's instincts. Can't be a Buddhist or Hindu without changing one's physiology.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The high water. Planks in the Piazza San Marco. The water is greener, more transparent in the canals. Staircases under water. The water tilts, rolls, laps, sways, slaps the stone.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Difference between cruelty and oppression. Nazis institutionalized cruelty---proclaimed evil (the SS death's-head insignia) ---maimed and tortured bodies, killed as a matter of policy / principle. Nothing was ever as cruel, as heartless as Auschwitz. But a Stalinist regime is more oppressive because more politicized. Less space for the private. A rhetoric of the good rather than of evil.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

André Breton in the early 1940s called up Meyer Schapiro to ask him if Newton's treatise on light was published in 1713; was exceedingly disappointed when Schapiro said it wasn't. Had wanted to put the date, as a signature, in painting.

::: p
A great subject for a novel: the temptation (corruption) of the good. Communism. Who will be the Solzhenitsyn of the "clercs communisants" \[["communizing clerks"]{.i}\] of the West?

Pound \> Lowell. Poetry should be a record of everything that comes into your head.

::: p
Visit with Joseph to \[[Ezra Pound's companion]{.i}\] Olga Rudge between 5 + 8 pm---252 San Gregorio (near Salute)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Olga Rudge always referred to Eliot as "Possum ..." Said that Pound was not contrite or penitent in the years between his release from St. Elizabeths and his death ... With a hint of tears in her eyes, she paused (only once) and said: "You know, Ezra was right. He was right. There's too much democracy. There's too much free speech ..." Said that \[[the American writer]{.i}\] Natalie Barney \[[in the margin:]{.i} knew Djuna Barnes\] spent most of the war in Rapallo, as Pound's guest ... In the tiny living room she has \[Henri\] Gaudier-Brzeska's big bust of Pound (on the floor) + the Wyndham Lewis drawing of Pound ... She insisted on the fact that Pound had a "Jewish first name" and didn't change it---"from the beginning---his first book---he signed himself 'Ezra Pound,' not 'E. Loomis Pound' or 'Loomis Pound'" and that the Loomises were a good family ("Look in the New York Social Register; you'll see many Loomises."). "A biblical name," I said. "That's right, a Jewish name. So if Ezra were an anti-Semite, as people say, he wouldn't have kept that Jewish name, now would he?"
:::

<?dp n="461" folio="445" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
She has an English accent. She says, "Capito?" after many sentences.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
" ... I'm like the ancient mariner," she said at the door. Joseph + I, in our coats, had been standing there for fifteen minutes while she went on without a stop. "Now what was his story about? Oh, yes, wasn't it something about a dead bird?" A closing line she must have used many times.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Sinyavsky not only brought out his family from the Soviet Union but hundreds of books and his black poodle Matilda. His wife has been back several times. A deal was made.

Creaking of the vaporetto piers, audible at night, when the boat unloads. Cooing of gulls riding the water. The damp smells. The left side of the basilica, black and white, that is better + more sharply seen at night than in the daytime. One sees too much in the day. The senses are sharper at night.

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/10/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle270){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title270}
:::

::: p
I'm reading \[Beckett's\] [Malone Dies]{.i}. That's prose that changes your life---that is, the way you write. How can anyone write the same in English after reading that?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
György Konrád: "L'écrivain qui a des positions militantes est un masochiste: il se prive de ses propres dons." \[["The writer who takes militant (political) positions is a masochist. He deprives himself of his own gifts."]{.i}\]
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
My political positions: all adversary. I am against (1) violence---+, in particular, colonialist wars and imperialist "interventions." Above all, against torture. (2) Sexual and racial discrimination. (3) The destruction of nature and the landscape (mental, architectural) of the past. (4) Whatever impedes or censors the {movement of people, art, ideas.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-c
{transport
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
(If I'm for anything, it is---simply---the decentralization of power. Plurality.)
:::

<?dp n="463" folio="447" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In short, the classic libertarian / conservative / radical position. I can be no more. I should not want to be more. I am not interested in "constructing" any new form of society, or joining any party. There is no reason for me to try to locate myself on either the left or the right---or to feel I should. That shouldn't be my language.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I feel guilty when I don't write, and that I don't write "enough." Why? What's this "guilt"? Joseph says the same of himself. Why should you feel guilty, I asked? "Because I used to write 20 good poems a year. Now I write only 7 or 10---though they're mostly better than what I used to write."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I accuse Joseph of doing his [Ninotchka]{.i} number \[[Ernst Lubitsch's 1939 comedy starring Greta Garbo as a Soviet agent]{.i}\]. (Which corner of the room is mine? \[[a paraphrased line from]{.i} (Which corner of the room is mine? \[a [paraphrased line from the film]{.i}\] etc.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Ripa de Meana: "Intellectuals in Europe have a Tabasco role." Are, inevitably, drawn to extreme positions.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The enemy is the thought: All problems are, finally, political problems. And, therefore, to be solved by political means.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"Nothing survives in the same form" (\[[the French Marxist literary critic]{.i}\] Pierre Macherey). Until recently the main form in which G\[ree\]k art survived in Western society was as a hegemonic ideal, through what Marx refers to as its capacity to "count as a norm and as an unattainable model."
:::

<?dp n="464" folio="448" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The following entry has a box drawn around it:]{.i}\] Art in the West: this once unwanted, but now accepted, telescope into ourselves.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In 17th + 18th century, Greek art functioned as essentially an attainable model. With the industrial revolution it begins to acquire attributes as unattainable (an "ideal"). Now the classics have been replaced by the study of national literatures. "The home of the totality has become literary criticism." (\[[The contemporary English critic and historian]{.i}\] Perry Anderson)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The formalist method: suitable for those ignorant of, or indifferent to, history. This, surely, is part of its appeal now. One doesn't need to be "learned" to understand a literary text or a painting, only intelligent. One doesn't need more than the work itself.
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/12/77](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle271){#e9781466802179_c14.html_title271}
:::

::: p
Churches: San Silvestro (in Piazza San S\[ilvestro\]). And Sant'Ignazio (in Piazza Sant'I\[gnazio\]. \[Rome\]): Baroque (Counter-Reformation, Jesuit) folie. The ceiling that is too high---the vertiginous scene---the false cupola (trompe l'oeil) that's right only from the center of the church! And now you have to pay 100 lire to see it!
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

<?dp n="465" folio="449" ?>

::: p
Beckett the opposite of Joyce. Get smaller, more precise, fussier, bleaker ... smaller and briefer. Could one be the opposite of Beckett now? That is, not Joyce. But not only bleak; and larger, and less [old]{.i}---
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
[\[Dated only "Note from 1977"\]]{.i}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
So far as it is really denied, death becomes the most important thing. (Like anything which is denied.) It is nowhere, and it is everywhere. While we deny death, the morbid has a supreme attraction for us. Perhaps because no transcendent source of values can anymore be detected, death (the extinction of consciousness) becomes a seal of value, of importance. (In a sense, only what concerns death has value.) This leads to both a promotion and a trivialization of the concept of death, which gives perhaps the deepest stimulus to the persistent iconography of violence + violent death in the artifacts of our culture. (The extraordinary frequency with which the plot of a serious contemporary novel turns on, or resolves itself, by a murder---compared with the extreme unlikelihood that the educated writers of vanguard fiction have ever been anywhere near a murder in their lives.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[1/17/78 NYC](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle273){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title273}
:::

::: p
[Tannhäuser]{.i} tonight at the Met \[[the Metropolitan Opera]{.i}\] (with \[[the American literary critic]{.i}\] Walter Clemons). The music is about sex---eroticism---voluptuousness. That's why one goes on loving Wagner. The stories of the operas, alas, are something else: the vulgarity; the kitsch problems (sex vs. soulfulness); the martial proto-Nazi volkishness. Nietzsche was right about Wagner---more right than he knew. And yet, and yet---that voluptuousness ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The Hebrew word for life, "chai," is spelled with two letters, chet and yod. These letters have numerical equivalents, chet, 8, and yod, 10, which add up to 18. Tradition of giving \$18 as a charitable donation. (To give a "chai" for ... to give a "triple chai" (\$54), one chai for my family, one chai for my friends, ... etc.) ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The need to find patterns, the need to pattern ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...

::: title-section1
[1/21/78](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle274){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title274}

::: p
Sex is getting a bad reputation. The 1960s---seemed like energy, joy, freedom from stuffy taboos, adventure. Now seems to many people more trouble than it's worth. A disappointment. Sex a sublimation of the desire to work. Sexual drive took them, into a wall ... Male homosexual "world" abandoned the gentle / bitchy homosexual (the "fairy," "fag," "fruit"---compulsively attending to his sexual needs)---+ gave itself over to lechery, vice, and sexual manias.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Distinction of "novel" from "romance" important through 19th c\[entury\] (\[Samuel\] Johnson's [Dictionary]{.i} defines a novel as "a small tale, generally of love"). Only rather recently did the term "novel" spread imperialistically to cover any long prose fiction.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Another way of thinking about why the questions, "Is this a novel?," "Is the novel dead?" are stupid.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

:::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[3/1/78 [\[or 3/9/78---the date is unclear in the notebook\]]{.i}](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle275){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title275}

::: p
I'm not thrilled anymore by literary criticism as auto-critique---the construction of methodologies, the deconstruction of texts. Criticism that is about itself.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
[Illness as Metaphor]{.i} is an attempt to "do" literary criticism in a new way but for a pre-modern purpose: to criticize the world.
:::

<?dp n="470" folio="454" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
It's also "against interpretation"---once again. With a subject, instead of a text.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I AM against turning illness into a "spiritual condition."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
About how the metaphoric understanding, and the moralization of a disease, belies the medical realities.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
So many modern ideas thought to be liberating to some class or relationship or just aspiration have turned out to be more enslaving than not.
:::

::: p-blanc

A story about the city. Two people traverse it, wander about---one looking for sexual adventure (prostitutes?), the other looking for an apartment. A. looks forward: desire. B. looks backward: regret, nostalgia for the lost space. Two experiences of time, two experiences of space (the labyrinth).

::: p
In 1874, Mallarmé started---and edited---a fashion magazine: [La Dernière Mode]{.i}. There he discovered (?), made his first experiments with layout and typography.

::: p
My ninth stay in Venice:
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-br
1961---with M\[other\], I\[rene\] (\[Hotel\] Luna; Hotel des Bains)\
1964---with D\[avid\] (Bob + Guido)---Luna\
1967---with D\[avid\] (Film Festival---Hotel Excelsior)\
1969---with C\[arlotta\] (\[Hotel\] Fenice)\
1972---with N\[icole\] (\[Hotel\] Gritti)\
1974---with N\[icole\] (Gossens apartment)\
1975---with N\[icole\] (Gritti)\
1977, Dec.---Joseph \[Brodsky\] (\[Hotel\] Europa)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
And he retired to Venice, to write a two-hundred-page poem on the sinking of the [Titanic]{.i}.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Imagination:---having many voices in one's head. The freedom for that.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
In every era, there are three teams of writers. The first team: those who have become known, gain "stature," become reference points for their contemporaries writing in the same language. (e.g. Emil Staiger, Edmund Wilson, V. S. Pritchett). The second team: international---those who become reference
<?dp n="476" folio="460" ?>
points for their contemporaries throughout Europe, the Americas, Japan, etc. (e.g. Benjamin). The third team: those who become reference points for successive generations in many languages (e.g. Kafka). I'm already on the first team, on the verge of being admitted to the second---want only to play on the third.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Dionysus was bisexual. (cf. \[[the Austrian-American psychoanalyst and writer]{.i}\] Helene Deutsch lecture)

 \

::: p
<span>
... Woman in Chicago (Jory Graham---columnist for [Sun-Times]{.i} ("A Time to Live")---sidekick in my cancer minstrel show---telling (on \[Irv\] Kup\[cinet\]'s show) how she was recently on a plane that lost an engine---how she panicked, though she tried to convince herself she was better off dying
<?dp n="477" folio="461" ?>
now, in 5 minutes, than going through the smelly, slow, agonizing hideous cancer death that awaits her soon---she wanted not to crash---she wanted her own death, the one she'd been working on, living with, getting reconciled (accustomed) to.`</span>`{=html}

::: p
<span>
Remember the exemplary career of Herwarth Walden ... Founded [Der Sturm]{.i} (published Kokoschka, Futurists, Kandinsky,
<?dp n="481" folio="465" ?>
Apollinaire) in 1910; married \[[the German Jewish poet and playwright]{.i}\] Else Lasker-Schüler \[in 1903\]. In 1932 stopped publication of [Der Sturm]{.i} + emigrated to USSR. Wrote novel there, "Neutral": never published. Arrested 31 March 1941 at the Hotel Metropol in Moscow; died in the hospital of the camp at Saratov.`</span>`{=html}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[8/20/78 NYC](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle295){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title295}
:::

::: p
<span>
Joseph B\[rodsky\] says he decides to write a poem---picks a subject and / or a model (a poet he admires); says to himself "I will write an Akhmatova poem" or " ... a Frost poem" or "an Auden poem" or a "\[[the Italian poet Eugenio]{.i}\] Montale poem" or a "Cavafy poem." The idea is to do it like the poet, only better.
<?dp n="484" folio="468" ?>
Of course, it never is like the model poet---that's a game one plays with oneself---if one is a real poet one can only write of one's own world.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A possible practice for the next year: a Borges story (the discovery of a play of Agathon \[[the classical Greek playwright whose works were supposedly lost when the great library of Alexandria burned in 48 BC]{.i}\]; a Calvino story; a Walser story; a Konrád story; a García Márquez story.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I've already done my Barthelme story---\[[SS's autobiographical short story]{.i}\] "Unguided Tour"---that is, written a better story than Barthelme. I should have admitted to myself that's what I was doing.

[11/17/78](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle297){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title297}
:::

::: p
After the party at Roger \[Straus\]'s for [I, etcetera]{.i}, published today---with Joseph at the coffee shop on the corner of 3rd Ave and 17th at midnight. "I realize that I've become a simpleton in the six years I've been in America. I'm no longer subtle, as I was in Russia. It's the American straightforwardness ... Everyone is positive here ... people trying to be helpful, kind, supportive ... explaining, making things clear."
:::

<?dp n="488" folio="472" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"You don't want a last line that's not an effect." Joseph's criticism of the last line of \[[SS's story]{.i}\] "Baby." I think he's wrong.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I get boosted by reading. But does this help to be subtle? Joseph: that comes only from other people.
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[12/27/78 Venice](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle300){#e9781466802179_c15.html_title300}
:::

::: p
Venice in December, a photographic negative of the sun-lit summer Venice. A kind of seeing-for-the-first-time.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Abstract Piazza San Marco---geometric---defined by borders of lights---space defined by thickness of light. Every figure is a silhouette.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In the vaporetto coming up from the Casino (Palazzo Vendramin ...): seeing nothing on either side of the boat. Looking into a brown-grey void.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The basilica from the top of the campanile---barely visible---The Doge's Palace like a Monet or a \[Georges\] Seurat drawing in the fog.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Winter Venice is metaphysical, structural, geometrical. Drained of color.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I've re-read \[Henry James's\] [The Golden Bowl]{.i}.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
To feel the pressures of consciousness, to be informed, to understand anything, one must be alone. Being with people, being alone---like breathing in and breathing out, systole and diastole. As long as I'm so afraid of being alone, I'll never be real. I'm in hiding from myself.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I act in haste---I reason for results---my intelligence is facile.
:::

<?dp n="491" folio="475" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The depression I feel when I'm alone is only the first layer. I can get beyond it if I don't panic. Sink down---let it happen. Listen to the words.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
(Talking to Bob \[[SS had by then made several trips to Japan and was contemplating a short book of her impressions]{.i}\] on the phone) Japan:
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Feudal society that has been modernized. Full of Western "signs" that don't signify anything in particular except modernity. Parody of Western "culture." National project: To "transcribe" (adapt, transmogrify) modern Western capitalism ... No law in our sense, but rather an immense system of accomodations, deferences, hierarchies. Consensus society---everyone is recuperable (leaders of \[[the extreme left student movement]{.i}\] Zengakuren in the late 60s are now important business executives) except for criminal underclass. Ritualized violence---in strikes, demonstrations against Narita \[[the construction of an airport there]{.i}\], etc.---which provide signs for new accommodations. Much energy, many signs---little substance. Big homosexual culture, 1000 gay bars, see Don \[[SS's friend in Tokyo, the American writer Donald Richie]{.i}\] and his German friend \[Eric Klestadt\]. In the green room of the Kabuki Theatre---prize is to know transvestite actors:---like ballet dancers or opera singers in the 19th c. Twelve department stores in Tokyo like Bloomingdale's: everything looks the same but it's really endlessly different.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

<?dp n="492" folio="476" ?>
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

[]{#e9781466802179_c16.html}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: chapter

::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[1/1/79 Asolo](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle302){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title302}
:::

::: p
Syberberg essay \[[SS had discovered the films of Hans-Jürgen Syberberg some years earlier, helped arrange of the U.S. distribution of]{.i} Hitler: A Film from Germany[, and by the beginning of 1979 had been planning this essay for several months.]{.i}\] Begin with the idea of "Trauerarbeit" \[["work of mourning"]{.i}\]
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
"It's worse than being a child."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The statue of St. Sebastian in the Duomo of Vicenza. (Altar nearest entrance on the left). The tradition of the beautiful nude youth transported from G\[reco\]-Rom\[an\] art into Christianity---was homoerotic---now an object of erotic contemplation by women mostly and men. The first three-dimensional St. Sebastian I've seen. The eroticism of this figure even more flagrant as a sculpture than as a painting ... The number of arrows (I've seen as few as two, as many as ten) and their placement.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
Again and again, I'm struck by the erotic obsessions that are so much in the foreground in Christianity. The Virgin---the
<?dp n="493" folio="477" ?>
breast of the Virgin / mother---the swooning woman---the beloved disciple leaning into Jesus' lap---the tortured male body, almost naked (Jesus, Sebastian)`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The poet Robert]{.i}\] Browning's word "asolare"
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
How modern were Ruskin's pleasures? Not our mix of enthusiasm and nostalgia (almost mourning) when we see Venice, Florence, Verona, etc. He was a discoverer. For whom? What does it mean to discover something already known? Known by whom?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The circle of removable plastic set in each of the two windows (Cipriani Hotel---Asolo). Like the removeable glass carreau in mullioned windows in the 19th century---but this is ugly, because it's an unbroken piece of glass---not mullioned.
:::
:::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[1/5/79 Paris](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle303){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title303}
:::

::: p
One to six with \[György\] Konrád (Scossa \> Stella \[[two cafés]{.i}\]). Stories about Eastern Europe. "L'étatisation des écrivains" \[["the subsuming of writers into the state"]{.i}\]. What it means to receive a prize ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
A story in this---about getting a prize?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
When I say, how can you compare the Russian empire with the American empire ("it's the same if I die from capitalist bombs or from communist bombs"---his Venice speech last December), he reminds me that I forget the periphery, the colonies of the American empire. Of course, if one compares New York with Moscow, there is no doubt that one is infinitely
<?dp n="494" folio="478" ?>
freer in New York---one is free, "tout court" \[["and that's that"]{.i}\]. But, Cambodia apart, there is nothing as cruel, as bloody in the Communist countries as what went on in Iran (the Shah's SAVAK \[[secret police]{.i}\]), in Nicaragua, + in Argentina right now. Intellectuals are not being murdered in Hungary, Poland, Czechoslovakia, etc. They are being wooed---or expelled.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

::: p

::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[1/14/79 London](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle305){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title305}
:::

::: p
Jonathan: "I wrote the medical book to make money and staple-gunned a few ideas in the middle."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I was praising \[Henry James's\] [The Golden Bowl]{.i}. J\[onathan\] said, "if you could have bolted Henry + William together you would get Proust."
:::

<?dp n="495" folio="479" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
J. talks of his long-projected book on 19th c. spiritualism. Description of cataleptic trance in "Maud" comes from Tennyson having read Harriet Martineau's [Letters on Mesmerism]{.i} (1845).
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[Henry\] James consulted \[[the British neurologist John]{.i}\] Hughlings Jackson in the 1890s in London for his migraines; Jackson told him about "temporal lobe epilepsy," during whose aura all sounds seem to stop, there is a strange smell, + one is overwhelmed by an awareness of unbearable evil. This is the origin of James's description of the governess' "hallucination" (?) in [The Turn of the Screw]{.i} ... [The Golden Bowl]{.i} is about observation, about seeing, about how one really never knows what another person feels.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Fascination in 19th c. with altered states of consciousness. Two traditions of interpreting these. (1) An alter ego---another state, side, aspect of me; exalted states (cf. Wordsworth, Dostoyevsky)---the egotistical interpretation. (2) An other world---the supernatural---spirits. (cf. Poe, \[E.T.A.\] Hoffmann)
:::

[1/27/79 Rome](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle307){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title307}
:::

::: p
Carmelo Bene's \[[production of Verdi's]{.i}\] [Otello]{.i}. The action is mostly played on a huge bed---begins with O\[tello\] strangling D\[esdemona\]. It is played inside the handkerchief. Everyone wears white. O. in brownface. People pass their hands over each other's faces, leaving them black. Voices are miked. Music of Verdi, Wagner, etc.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-blanc
 \

Language as a found object: \[[the Argentine writer Manuel]{.i}\] Puig. He can't create his own language. It's all found. He's an extraordinary mime---has converted his liability as a writer into a system.

:::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[2/8/79](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle309){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title309}
:::

::: p
The aura around each thing.
:::

::: p
Respect it---pause a moment---before you grasp something
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Aesthetic space: \[[the eighteenth-century French painter Jean-Baptiste-Simeon]{.i} \] Chardin, \[[the American experimental filmmaker]{.i} \] Jack Smith
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
America + Western Europe are growing apart---becoming as different as they were in the 1950s. Western Europe has chosen Social Democracy (whatever the party in power calls itself), America has rejected it. Events of the 70s: 1) the discrediting of utopian communism as a plausible anchor-belief for intellectuals + artists; 2) the Euro-ization of the Western European countries; 3) the collapse of American imperialist ideology + growing cultural / political isolationism of the U.S.

::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[2/11/79](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle310){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title310}
:::

::: p
Conversation with Enzensberger (lunch in Chinatown): Darwin as an alternative to Hegel. Hegelianism assumes the biological + the historical are two different processes. But maybe the historical proceeds naturally. An evolution, but one that can't be predicted. (What was attractive about Hegelianism was its notion of the [irony]{.i} of history.) Nobody has thought about Darwin\['s\] implications for 50 years in Germany, E. says. Survival of the fittest is mistrusted as survival of the strongest.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Wants to write a free kind of essay. Cites Heine as a model. I mention Lucretius---he agrees.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Canetti:
:::

\

::::::: blocktext
::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
biologistic model ([Crowds + Power]{.i}). No "history" in the Hegelian sense
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
one of the great death-haters of the 20th century
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
not Eurocentric. Cites Chinese or Arab thought---not as something from another culture to be "understood," but because it's true
:::

::: p-indent-inverse-blocktext
not a reductionist---never asks what makes an idea possible, but: "is it true?"
:::
:::::::

\

::: p
The strength, independence---+ marginality of Canetti's work. Was supported in the late 1930s + '40s by the Guggenheim Foundation (E. says. True?) Knew---had affair with Iris Murdoch. E. was introduced to Canetti in London by Ingeborg Bachmann ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
Canetti: Avidity, appetite, craving, longing, yearning, insatiability, rapture, inclination. Is this the life of the mind?
<?dp n="499" folio="483" ?>
`</span>`{=html}
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[2/25/79](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle314){#e9781466802179_c16.html_title314}
:::

::: p
\[[The American choreographer]{.i}\] Twyla Tharp reconciles me to being a woman + to being an American ... Non-sexist dancing---strong women with their own energy, subjects not objects, playful with men---not afraid of them ... Use of American vernacular movement (from Mack Sennett comedy, Fred Astaire, black disco dancers), of American energy. Constant contact with the floor, which is not just something to leave---as in \[[the work of the choreographer George]{.i}\] Balanchine. Slapping the floor---falling on it flat out, trying to get up, [hugging]{.i} it.
:::

<?dp n="500" folio="484" ?>

::: p

::: p
A failure of nerve. About writing. (And about my life---but never mind.) I must write myself out of it.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
If I am not able to write because I'm afraid of being a bad writer, then I must be a bad writer. At least I'll be writing.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Then something else will happen. It always does.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I must write every day. Anything. Everything. Carry a notebook with me at all times, etc.

::: p-blanc

I read my bad reviews. I want to go to the bottom of it---this failure of nerve.

I am mad, quite mad---and perhaps one can write about that. No one has noticed. My prowess in disguising it. I wander about the apartment, slyly rummaging ... No place is the right place for my feet. Time is speeded-up. I lie down, I get up, I pace, I lie down, I sleep, I get up, and so on.

::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[1/24/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle328){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title328}
:::

::: p
A story called "Fear of War"
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Lunch with \[[the American writer]{.i}\] Joyce Carol Oates, her husband Ray Smith, + Stephen K\[och\]. Stephen speaks of his psychological weather---there's always the weather, he says. Not true, say I. But there's always the sky, says Stephen. Who goes out? I reply. Not I. I don't have weather. I have central heating. My central heating is Western civilization---my books + pictures + records.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joyce writes all the time. She can meditate while writing. She says she has no feelings. What's the point of feeling anxiety? "I'll probably go to my death as on a conveyor belt," she said. Stephen said she had a mystical experience at 30---in London: it lasted twenty minutes ...

::: p-blanc

::: p
Two ideas---"the idea of the artistic vocation, of the artist who has renounced worldly ambitions in order to dedicate himself / herself to values that cannot be realized by commercial society" and the idea of cultural or artistic iconoclasm, the artist's alienation from society, art as transgression, adversary art, avant-garde---these have been conflated. Both seem irrelevant or unreal to most artists now. But are scorned by art critics. But they're not the same.

Essay: (?)
:::

::: p
The Aphorism. The Fragment---all of these are "notebook-thinking"; are produced by the idea of keeping a notebook.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
One could trace history of thought / art in relation to the forms of transcription: letter manuscript notebook.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The notebook has become an art form (Rilke, Lizzie's book \[[Sleepless Nights]{.i}\]), a thought-form (Barthes), even a philosophical form (Lichtenberg, Nietzsche, Wittgenstein, Cioran, Canetti).
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Decline of the letter, the rise of the notebook! One doesn't write to others any more; one writes to oneself.
