:::

::: p
Why? Parsimony? Don't squander one's pretty phrases, one's wisdom on someone else---a distant recipient who may not have the courtesy to save the letter.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Save it for yourself!
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Hoarding ideas.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The [persona]{.i} of a notebook is different. More insolent (let's not think about the whiners!)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
[Aphorism]{.i}. Aphorism features aristocratic pessimism \[[In the margin:]{.i}\] scorn, cool. Alt\[ernative\]: Aphorism features pessimism and rapidity.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[Canetti's\] aphorisms are concentrated thought.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] Reading Canetti recalls Montaigne, Gracian, Chamfort, Lichtenberg, and (among contemporaries) Cioran---the same wisdom, essentially: a wisdom of pessimism.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Aphorisms are rogue ideas.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Aphorism is aristocratic thinking: this is all the aristocrat is willing to tell you; he thinks you should get it fast, without spelling out all the details. Aphoristic thinking constructs thinking as an obstacle race: the reader is expected to get it fast, and move on. An aphorism is not an argument; it is too well-bred for that.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
<span>
To write aphorisms is to assume a mask---a mask of scorn, of superiority. Which, in one great tradition, conceals (shapes) the aphorist's secret pursuit of spiritual salvation. The [paradoxes]{.i}
<?dp n="529" folio="513" ?>
of salvation. We know at the end, when the aphorist's amoral, light point-of-view self-destructs.`</span>`{=html}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Example: Gracian, who concludes his book on the courtier by observing that the courtier must, logically, be a saint; or Wilde, whose brilliance seems much of the time to be Nietzsche minus the tragic sense, ends with the wretched mortifying wisdom of [De Profundis]{.i}.
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[4/29/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle344){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title344}
:::

::: p
The quotation \< \> the trip
:::

::: p
Silence
:::

::: p
The three ideas with which I have the world.
:::

::: p
Each one needs the other two.
:::

::: p
I can't replace one without changing the other two.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] [Trip to Hanoi]{.i}, "Unguided Tour," "Project for a Trip to China," "Debriefing"
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Fictions constructed out of quotations---
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The world perceived as an anthology of quotations (the essays on photography)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Stories that end with an affirmation of silence \[---\] "Dr. Jekyll," and [The Benefactor]{.i}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] [Death Kit]{.i} ends with a vision of death as a museum of quotations. Theme of quotation in the essays on Godard + Benjamin, + in "Project for a Trip to China"
:::

<?dp n="530" folio="514" ?>

::: p
Quotation, for me, is my continuation of the idea of "the fragment"---the first discovery of the modernist sensibility {Schlegel brothers \[[August and Friedrich]{.i}\], Novalis}
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In Russia, people wait for the poet to have the last word. (Nowhere does literature matter so much.)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
"No, tell me first," said the Hungarian exile, "between truth and justice which would you choose?"
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
"Truth."
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
"Right," he said.
:::

::: p-indent-inverse
Tout est là \[["It's all there"]{.i}\]
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
One [must]{.i} oppose communism: it asks us to lie---the sacrifice of the intellect (and the freedom to create) in the name of justice. (And, finally, order.) Think of \[[the Russian novelist and, having become an apologist for Stalin, publicist Ilya]{.i}\] Ehrenburg, who knowingly sacrificed his talent.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Communism means the creation of a much more oppressive bureaucracy than capitalism.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
There is no such thing as communism. Only national socialism. ---That's what won. (Nationalism the most imp\[ressive\] political force of the 20th C.). The fascist language was defeated---the communist language survived, + became the rhetoric (+ the flag of convenience) of most new nationalisms, ex-colonialized peoples.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Hitler lost. But national socialism---small n, small s---won.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
You can't become English, French, German; you are ... But you become an American.
:::

::: p
An invented, not a natural country.
:::

<?dp n="531" folio="515" ?>

::: p
A country in which every relation is a contract, including familyship, and may, at the displeasure of either of the participants, be broken. Indeed, should be.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[The contemporary American satiric essayist]{.i}\] Fran Lebowitz's mother: "But everything you say is a promise." The Jewish-Protestant view.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
In Italy, a promise is no more than a plan, a statement of intention. It's understood that one can change one's mind.
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[4/30/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle345){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title345}
:::

::: p
An enthusiast modernist? An involuntary modernist?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Symbolist novel: examination of the inside of a fantasy
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The first thing to understand is that Americans have never suffered. That they don't know about suffering. (Me last night at dinner \[with\] Heberto + Belkis Padilla \[[the exiled Cuban poet and his wife]{.i}\].
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Making lists of words, to thicken my active vocabulary. To have puny, not just little, hoax, not just trick, mortifying, not just embarrassing, bogus, not just fake.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I could make a story out of puny, hoax, mortifying, bogus. They [are]{.i} a story.
:::
:::::::::::::

::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[5/2/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle346){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title346}
:::

::: p
Story about a poet (Joseph!) so much less, morally, than his work
:::

<?dp n="532" folio="516" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Joseph defending the Shah \[of Iran\], and torture, yesterday at lunch (The Silver Palace \[[a New York Chinese restaurant where SS and Brodsky ate often]{.i}\]) w\[ith\] Stephen + Natasha \[Spender\], and David. And now I re-read \[[Brodsky's poem]{.i}\] "Lullaby of Cape Cod."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::
:::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[5/6/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle347){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title347}
:::

::: p
Yes, an essay on aphoristic thinking! Another ending, wrapping up. "Notes on Notes."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
With the (1943) epigraph of Canetti. "The great writers of aphorisms read as if they had all known each other very well."
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
One wonders why. Can it be that the literature of aphorisms teaches us the sameness of wisdom (as anthropology teaches us the diversity of culture)? The wisdom of pessimism. Or should we rather conclude that the form of the aphorism, of abbreviated or condensed or rogue thought, is a historically-colored voice which, when adopted, inevitably suggests certain attitudes; is the vehicle of a common thematics?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The traditional thematics of the aphorist: the hypocrises of societies, the vanities of human wishes, the shallowness + deviousness of women; the sham of love; the pleasures (and necessity) of solitude; + the intricacies of one's own thought processes.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
All the great aphorists struggle to assume the burden of pessimism, of disillusionment---some with more mildness (less ferocity) than others.
:::

<?dp n="533" folio="517" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
All note the mendacities + hypocrises of social life. And many of the great aphorists (Chamfort, Kraus) are not just condescending to but contemptuous of women; many are fascinated by their own mental processes + mental process in general (Lichtenberg, Wittgenstein).
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] Taste for paradox, hyperbole
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Aphoristic thinking is impatient thinking: by its very brevity or concentratedness, it presupposes a superior standard ...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The characteristic arrogance of aphoristic thinking. A pose? A spur?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
... The most notable exception (to the fact that most of the great aphorists have been pessimists), Lichtenberg, \[who\] followed English rather than European models of scorn for human folly: he regarded himself as an adopted Englishman, and declared common sense, which he considered characteristically English, as the mind's greatest virtue.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] The English are cooler (Wilde, Auden)
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
\[[In the margin:]{.i}\] The aphorist's favorite subject: himself; Lichtenberg not actively misogynistic.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Another exception among the great aphorists is \[[the Mauritian writer and painter]{.i} Malcolm de\] Chazal---neither optimistic not pessimistic. Because he is a naturalist.
:::

<?dp n="534" folio="518" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Canetti shares in the scorn for human folly of the main European tradition---the misanthropy, and misogyny, endemic to the aphoristic tradition.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Aphorism is generally regarded as a product of detachment, a kind of superciliousness of the mind. In Canetti, as in Cioran, aphorism is the skill (product) appropriate to the over-passionate mind of the eternal student.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
Montaigne, who created the modern essay---also an aphorist?
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
...
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The writing doctors ...
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::: section1
::: title-section1
[5/9/80](#e9781466802179_toc01.html_toctitle348){#e9781466802179_c17.html_title348}
:::

::: p
Nijinsky was not an intellectual. He was an idea. (\[[The American ballet critic]{.i}\] A. \[[Arlene]{.i}\] Croce)
:::

::: p
Canetti essay---it's a piece of fiction about "Canetti"---my Kien \[[the tragic hero of Canetti's]{.i} Auto-da-Fé\]. In [that]{.i} sense, about me.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
The only review of [Under the Sign of Saturn]{.i} would be the eighth essay---an essay describing me as I have described them. The pathos of intellectual avidity, the collector (mind as every-thing), melancholy & history, arbitrating the moral claim versus aestheticism, and so forth. The intellectual as an impossible project.
:::

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
If there is a unifying theme of my work it is naïve. The theme of moral seriousness, of passionateness. A mood, a tone.
:::

<?dp n="535" folio="519" ?>

::: p-blanc
 \
:::

::: p
I must give up writing essays because that inevitably becomes a demagogic activity. I seem to be the bearer of certainties that I [don't]{.i} possess---am not near to possessing.
:::
